SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
Taxilanes G and L at the Honolulu International Airport HNL were constructed in
the 1970 s for Group III aircrafts As part of the airport modernization plan the taxilanes
will be widened and the separation distance will be increased to accommodate larger
Group IV and Group V aircrafts in association with the construction of the new Mauka
Concourse Eventually the taxilanes will be straightened between the Interisland Terminal
apron and Taxiway A
The results of our pavement analyses indicated that the existing Taxilanes G and L
pavements are not capable of supporting Group IV and Group V aircrafts in their present
conditions and the pavements will need to be strengthened Both the options of
pavement overlay with asphalt concrete AC and complete pavement reconstruction had
been studied and were determined to be feasible alternatives The pavement
rehabilitation strategy will need to be evaluated and selected based on the final site
grading plans and other non engineering design factors such as construction schedule
impact to airport operations and project cost
For the interim design the existing Taxilane G could be rehabilitated with AC
overlay depending on the layout plan and the factors described above However
Taxilane L will likely need to be reconstructed in order to meet the minimum separation
requirements between adjacent parallel Taxilanes for Group V aircrafts as specified in the
FAA Advisory Circular
The pavement conditions and pavement thickness in the first 500 feet pavement
area identified as TL G L IIT500 on Plate 2 from the mauka end of Taxilanes G and L
were similar to Taxilane G In the pavement evaluation and analyses this 500 foot long
area was included as part of the Taxilane G pavement design
area in front of the Interisland Terminal appeared to be in relatively good condition The
estimated remaining life of this pavement should be at least 10 years depending on the
actual aircraft traffic Therefore no immediate pavement rehabilitation work is necessary
SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
Page v
Hawaii California
The existing Portland cement concrete PCC pavement in the boarding gate area
in front of the Interisland Terminal appeared to be in good condition It is our
understanding that no significant increase of heavy aircraft traffic is expected for the IIT
The estimated remaining life of this PCC pavement is about 14 years Therefore
pavement rehabilitation work is not required at this time
The pavement recommendations presented in this report are intended for use by
the project team in the planning design of the HNL Terminal Modernization Program and
should not be used at this time for final design The grading plans for the project could
affect the final pavement design recommendations and the AC overlay construction
alternative may not be feasible The final pavement rehabilitation strategy may include a
combination of grinding and overlay and the pavement design recommendations may
need to be modified In addition the pavement conditions will deteriorate from continuous
usage and environmental factors Therefore we should be provided with an opportunity to
review and finalize the pavement design recommendations presented herein at the
appropriate time The pavement design recommendations for the Mauka Concourse
taxilanes and apron are presented separately in our report entitled Pavement Evaluation
and Recommendations New Mauka Concourse Honolulu International Airport Honolulu
Oahu Hawaii W O 6231 00 dated
The text of this report should be referred to for detailed discussions and specific
design recommendations
END OF SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC

SECTION 1 GENERAL
1 1
Introduction
This report contains our findings on the evaluation of the existing taxilane and
apron pavements and recommendations on the pavement rehabilitation and
strengthening for Taxilane G TL G Taxilane L TL L and the Taxilanes G and
the Island of Oahu in the State of Hawaii The project location and general vicinity are
shown on the Project Location Map Plate 1
This report summarizes the findings and pavement design recommendations
derived from our field exploration laboratory testing and engineering analyses The
pavement recommendations presented herein are intended for use by the project team
in the planning design of the HNL Terminal Modernization Program and should not be
used for final design because the final grading plan of the pavement area was not
available at the time this report is prepared The proposed AC overlay construction may
not be feasible The final pavement rehabilitation strategy may include complete
pavement reconstruction or a combination of grinding and overlay The pavement
design recommendations presented herein may need to be modified for final design In
addition the pavement conditions will deteriorate from continuous usage and
environmental factors Therefore we should be provided with an opportunity to review
and finalize the pavement design recommendations presented herein at the appropriate
time
1 2
Project Considerations
The project involves the evaluation of the existing asphalt concrete AC
evaluation is the Portland cement concrete PCC pavement in the boarding gate area
in front of the Interisland Terminal IIT The locations of the taxilanes and aprons
evaluated by this study are shown on the General Site Plan Plate 2
SECTION 1 GENERAL

Page 2
Hawaii California
Taxilanes G and L at the Honolulu International Airport were constructed in the
1970 s for Group III aircrafts As part of the airport modernization plan the taxilanes will
be widened and separation will be increased to accommodate larger Group IV and
Group V aircrafts in association with the construction of the new Mauka Concourse
Eventually the taxilanes will be straightened between the Interisland Terminal apron
and Taxiway A
The pavement design recommendations for the Mauka Concourse taxilanes and
apron are presented separately in our report entitled Pavement Evaluation and
Recommendations New Mauka Concourse Honolulu International Airport Honolulu
Oahu Hawaii W O 6231 00 dated
It is desired to reassess the current taxilane pavement strength service life based
on the aircraft traffic schedule projected for the Year 2018 the new falling weight
deflectometer FWD nondestructive testing and the additional borings to confirm the
existing pavement composition and thicknesses Critical aspects of the design would
include the possibility of strengthening of the existing pavement section by overlay with
asphalt concrete to avoid disruption to airport operations and costly and time
consuming pavement reconstruction
1 3
Purpose and Scope
The purpose of our geotechnical and pavement engineering services has been to
evaluate the existing pavement and subsurface conditions at the site and to develop
pavement rehabilitation strategy including overlay design and pavement reconstruction
recommendations for the taxilanes and aprons Our work was performed in general
accordance with our revised proposal dated August 12 2009 The scope of work
included the following tasks and work efforts
1
Application for excavation permits from the applicable agencies within the
airport and coordination of site access and underground utility toning by
our engineer or geologist
2
Preparation and submittal of an accident prevention plan with activity
hazard analysis in support of our field exploration activities
SECTION 1 GENERAL
W O 6213 00

Page 3
Hawaii California
3
Mobilization and demobilization of a truck mounted drill rig and
two operators to the project site and back
4
Drilling and sampling of extending to depths of up to
5 feet below the existing ground surface at selected project site locations
5
Performance of FWD testing by All About Pavements Inc API
subconsultant to Geolabs Inc
6
Evaluation of the FWD deflection measurements to determine suitable
parameters for pavement evaluation based on Federal Aviation
Administration FAA guidelines
7
Coordination of the field exploration and logging of the borings by our field
engineer or geologist
8
Laboratory testing of selected soil samples obtained during the field
exploration as an aid in classifying the materials and evaluating their
engineering properties
9
Analyses of the field and laboratory data to formulate geotechnical
recommendations for the pavement strengthening reconstruction design
10
Preparation of this report summarizing our work on the project and
presenting our findings and pavement design recommendations
11
Coordination of our overall work on the project by our engineer
12
Quality assurance of our work and client design team consultation by our
principal engineer
13
Miscellaneous work efforts such as drafting word processing and clerical
support

SECTION 3 CORINGS AND EXPLORATORY BORINGS
We explored the subsurface conditions at the site by drilling and sampling
twenty two 22 borings designated as Boring Nos 1 through 22 to depths of about
4 to 5 feet below the existing ground surface The approximate boring locations are
shown on the Overall Boring Location Plan Plate 3 and on Boring Location Plans
Plates 4 1 and 4 2
Our geologist classified the materials encountered in the borings by visual and
textural examination in the field and monitored the drilling operations on a
near continuous basis Soils were classified in general conformance with the Unified
Soil Classification System as shown on the Soil Log Legend Plate A
Relatively undisturbed soil samples were obtained in general accordance with
ASTM D 3550 Ring Lined Barrel Sampling of Soils by driving a 3 inch OD Modified
California sampler with a 140 pound hammer falling 30 inches Some samples were
obtained from the borings in general accordance with ASTM D 1586 Penetration Test
and Split Barrel Sampling of Soils by driving a 2 inch OD standard penetration sampler
using the same hammer and drop The blow counts needed to drive the sampler the
second and third 6 inches of an 18 inch drive are shown as the penetration resistance
on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths
The logs of the borings were prepared based on the field logs and laboratory test
results and are presented on Plates A 1 through A 22 in Appendix A The logs of the
previous borings B 3H and B 4H drilled by Geolabs in 2002 are presented on
Plates A 23 and A 24 respectively
END OF CORINGS AND EXPLORATORY BORINGS
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
Page 7
Hawaii California
SECTION 4 LABORATORY TESTING
In order to further classify the subsurface materials encountered during our field
exploration the following laboratory tests were conducted on the soil samples
recovered from the exploratory borings
Moisture Content ASTM D 2216 and Unit Weight ASTM D 2937
determinations were performed on selected soil samples as an aid in the
classification and evaluation of soil properties The test results are
presented on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths
Four 4 Atterberg Limits tests ASTM D 4318 were performed to evaluate
the liquid and plastic limits and to aid in soil classification The test results
are summarized on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths
Graphic presentation of the test results is provided on Plate B 1
Twelve 12 Sieve Analysis tests ASTM C 136 D 1140 were performed
on selected soil samples to evaluate the gradation characteristics of the
soils and to aid in soil classification Graphic presentation of the grain size
distribution is presented on Plates B 2 through B 4
Four 4 laboratory California Bearing Ratio CBR tests ASTM D 1883
were performed on bulk samples of the near surface soils to evaluate the
pavement support characteristics and swelling potential of the soils The
test results are presented on Plates B 5 through B 8
END OF LABORATORY TESTING
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
Page 8

SECTION 5 VISUAL PAVEMENT CONDITION SURVEY
A visual condition survey of the pavements in the project area was performed on
by a Geolabs engineer During the field survey our engineer
walked along the taxilanes and aprons recorded approximate pavement distress
locations and determined pavement distress types through visual classification
The visual classification was performed in general accordance with the
definitions described in the Asphalt Distress Manual and Concrete Distress Manual
dated June 1997 prepared by the Maintenance and Preservation Division FL P of the
Facilities Technology Laboratory FL U S Army Construction Engineering Research
Laboratories USACERL The most common pavement distress types and their
definitions are presented in the table below
ASPHALT PAVEMENT DISTRESSES
Alligator Cracking
Fatigue
Alligator cracks are a series of interconnecting cracks caused by
fatigue failure of the asphaltic concrete surface under repeated
traffic loading
Block Cracking
Block cracks are interconnected cracks that divide the pavement
into approximately rectangular pieces
Depression
Depressions are localized pavement surface areas with
elevations slightly lower than those of the surrounding pavement
Edge Cracking
Edge cracks are cracks parallel to and usually within 1 to 1 5 feet
of the outer edge of the pavement
Longitudinal
Cracking
Longitudinal cracks are cracks parallel to the pavement s
centerline or laydown direction
Transverse
Cracking
Transverse cracks are cracks extending across the pavement at
approximately right angles to the pavement centerline or direction
of laydown
Patch
Patch is an area of pavement that has been replaced with new
material to repair the existing pavement
Pothole
Potholes are small bowl shaped usually less than 30 inches in
diameter depressions in the pavement surface
Polished
Aggregate
Polished Aggregate is the portion of aggregate of a pavement
extending above the asphalt is either very small or there are no
rough or angular aggregate particles to provide good skid
resistance
SECTION 5 VISUAL PAVEMENT CONDITION SURVEY
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
Page 9
Hawaii California
ASPHALT PAVEMENT DISTRESSES
Rutting
A rut is a surface depression in the wheel paths
Weathering
Raveling
Weathering and raveling are wearing away of the pavement
surface due to a loss of asphalt or tar binder and dislodged
aggregate particles
CONCRETE PAVEMENT DISTRESSES
Corner Break
A corner break is a crack that intersects the joint at a distance
less than 6 feet on each side measured from the corner of the
slab
Joint Load
Transfer System
Associated
Deterioration
This distress develops as a transverse crack a short distance
from a transverse joint at the end of joint dowels
Longitudinal
Cracking
Longitudinal cracks are cracks parallel to the pavement
centerline
Transverse and
Diagonal
Cracking
Transverse and diagonal cracks are linear cracks extending
across the pavement at angles to the pavement centerline
Joint Spalling
Joint spalling is the cracking breaking or chipping of the slab
edges within 2 feet of the joint
Joint Seal
Damage
Joint seal damage exists when incompressible materials and or
water can infiltrate into the joints
Joint Faulting
Joint faulting is a difference of elevations across a joint
Popout
A popout is a small piece of concrete that breaks loose from the
surface due to freeze thaw action expansive aggregates and or
non durable materials
Scaling Map
Cracking and
Crazing
Map cracking and crazing is a series of fine cracks that extend
only into the upper surface of the slab surface Scaling may be
caused by deicing salts improper construction freeze thaw
cycles and poor aggregate

the project site area are summarized in the following table

Only minor visual distress patterns were observed on the TL G and TL L
pavement surface However repair work was performed on Taxilane L in 2008 that
included grinding the top 2 5 inches of the old AC and replacing with 2 5 inches of new
AC pavement In addition a pavement area about 40 feet wide and 600 feet long on
TL G between the two middle cross access roadways was used as a test section for the
TL L repair construction and received the same construction sections as for TL L
Therefore pavement distresses may not be visible at this time We understand that
rutting was noticed in 2008 on the TL L pavement within the wheel paths of the B767
aircraft approximate 17 feet from the centerline of the taxilane In addition a
pavement failure on TL G was reported recently in April of 2010 We do not have any
details of the failure and repair work at this time Depending on the distress types
severity and extent it could indicate localized subgrade problems
For the pavement in front of the IIT the AC pavement distress level is estimated
at low to medium severity The most significant distress is small cracks along the
taxilanes In addition minor rutting was observed in front of the Hawaiian Airline
Maintenance Facility The PCC pavement in front of the IIT Building is generally in good
condition Only minor distresses involving small corner breaks and small cracks were
observed

SECTION 6 NONDESTRUCTIVE DEFLECTION TESTING NDT
A falling weight deflectometer FWD owned and operated by API was used to
measure the pavement deflections on the taxilane and apron pavements on
October 24 and 25 2009 The weight was dropped from three different heights at each
test location to generate dynamic forces of approximately 19 23 and 28 kips
Deflections were measured at a spacing of about every 100 feet along the
pavement area The approximate starting point Station 0 00 was located at the
Taxiway A end for the TL G and TL L measurements and at the northern intersection of
TL G and TL L for the IIT apron The stationing increased toward the north
H 1 Nimitz Highway The results of the NDT deflection measurements were
presented in the API s report dated March 1 2010 which is included herewith as
Appendix D
The FWD deflection data were normalized to a 40 kip test load in order to
compare with the deflection data recorded in Plates 5 1 and 5 2
show the plot of maximum deflections normalized to 40 kip load along TL G and TL L
respectively The pavement deflection plot on the IIT apron pavement is presented on
Plate 5 3
The following is a summary of the deflection measurements normalized to 40 kip
load TL G L IIT500 is designated as the pavement area from the mauka end of
TL G TL L extending about 500 feet north toward the Nimitz Highway into the
Taxilanes TL G and L and the Interisland Terminal IIT apron see Plate 2

As shown in the above table the currently measured pavement deflection on TL
L was significantly higher than the previous value indicating that the taxilane pavement
had deteriorated since the last deflection measurements in 2001 even though pavement
repair work was done in 2008 The deflection on the TL G pavement was also higher
but the increase was not as large as on TL L We understand that TL G is normally
used by arrival traffic at which time the aircraft weight is usually lighter The pavement
pavement deflections on TL G L IIT AC apron and IIT PCC apron were generally
similar to the previous measurements within about one standard deviation
As shown on Plate 5 1 the pavement deflections were slightly lower at the first
600 feet near Taxiway A than the remainder of the taxilane There was one unusually
high deflection about 115 mils and one unusually low deflection about 15 mils in the
measurements It is our opinion that these two measurements would likely be an
anomaly in the system or the sensor could be located in a patch area trench backfill
These two data points were excluded from the analyses
The deflections on TL L as shown on Plate 5 2 were relatively uniform The high
and low values appeared to be within the expected normal ranges As shown on
a high deflection of over 100 mils The pavement deflections became relatively uniform
within the TL G L IIT apron area in front of the Interisland Terminal

SECTION 7 PAVEMENT AND SUBSURFACE CONDITIONS
7 1
Existing Pavement Thickness
The following table is a summary of the existing AC and PCC pavement
structural sections encountered in the current field exploration program along with those
discovered in the previous studies The numbers in the highlighted area bold letters
are the representative 84th percentile values used in the subsequent pavement
evaluation and analyses
As shown on the table the measured thickness of the aggregate base course
appeared to be highly variable Please notice that the aggregate base rock and the
underlying basaltic and coralline gravel fill were very similar in appearance and
engineering properties The drilling was done with 4 inch auger and PQ coring It was
very difficult to visually determine the exact boundary between the two layers in the
field In addition as indicated later in this report the modulus of the base rock and the
subgrade material was very close Therefore the exact layer thickness is not critical in
the analyses For the AC and PCC thicknesses we selected the 84th percentile values
for a slightly conservative approach

7 2
Subsurface Conditions
In general the exploratory borings encountered granular fill material below the
pavement structural section The fill consisted of very dense sandy gravel basaltic and
coralline and dense to very dense coralline silty sand to depths of about 3 to over
5 feet Medium stiff highly plastic silty clay CH was encountered in previous borehole
B 3H at a depth of about 3 feet The granular fill or silty clay soil was underlain by hard
volcanic tuff to a depth of about 37 to 39 feet Below the tuff was very dense sandy
coralline gravel We encountered groundwater at depths of about 13 and 19 5 feet in the
two deep borings drilled at the new Mauka Concourse site at the time of our field
exploration
In accordance with the 1970 construction plans the pavements in the TL G TL L
course The materials below the base course should consist of 12 inch aggregate
subbase and 24 inch granular fill Since the base materials are similar to the underlying
granular fill it was very difficult to estimate the exact boundary between the two layers
during our field exploration
END OF PAVEMENT AND SUBSURFACE CONDITIONS

SECTION 8 DESIGN AIRCRAFT TRAFFIC
The design aircraft traffic was estimated based on the anticipated Year 2018
flight schedule which indicated that A350 aircrafts would embark from the new
Mauka Concourse The A350 aircraft is currently under development and the design
information is not yet available for use by the public Based on discussion with the
project team and following the FAA design guidelines we had analyzed two aircraft
traffic scenarios in the pavement design 1 replacing all the A350 aircrafts with the
B777 aircrafts and 2 replacing half the A350 aircrafts with the B777 aircrafts and the
other half with B787 aircrafts The results of the pavement engineering analyses
indicated that Scenario 1 would yield a slightly thicker pavement section and therefore
are presented in this report as the recommended pavement design In addition the
Year 2018 aircraft traffic was assumed as the average for a 20 year design life between
2010 and 2030 Therefore no annual growth rate was included in the pavement design
analyses
The total estimated Mauka Concourse and Interisland Terminal aircraft traffic
However only the aircraft traffic for IIT was used to evaluate the IIT PCC apron
The aircraft traffic forecast departures used in the pavement design is
summarized in table format and presented in Appendix E Commuter planes which are
light do not contribute significant damage to the pavements and for this reason are not
included in the pavement design
For pavement evaluation and overlay design purposes aircraft traffic can be
depicted in terms of the equivalent annual departures of the design aircraft in this
project the B777 300ER substitute to A350 This aircraft was selected as the design
aircraft because it has the heaviest wheel load i e causing most of the pavement
damage The procedure contained in the FAA Advisory Circular
combining the aircraft mix into a single design aircraft was followed to estimate the
SECTION 8 DESIGN AIRCRAFT TRAFFIC
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
Page 17
Hawaii California
Equivalent Annual Departures of the design aircraft B777 and the results are also
presented in Appendix E
We were informed by the Hawaii DOT Airports Division that the aircraft traffic
forecast
used
to
develop
the
pavement
design
recommendations
in
our
draft report and presented in Appendix E had been changed slightly
In general the departures of A330 and A350 aircraft from the Mauka Concourse
increase from 7 to 10 and from 6 to 7 respectively
Additional engineering analyses were performed using the updated aircraft traffic
data The results of our analyses showed that the increase in pavement thickness due
to the increase in aircraft traffic was generally less than two tenths of an inch In
addition the aircraft traffic schedule was just an estimate Therefore it is our opinion
that the pavement design recommendations presented in our draft
report remain valid for this final report

SECTION 10
AIRCRAFT AND PAVEMENT CLASSIFICATION NUMBERS
The aircraft classification number ACN and pavement classification number
PCN are used to report the load bearing capacity of airport pavements as required by
the International Civil Aviation Organization ICAO In accordance with FAA Advisory
Circular ACN is defined as a number that expresses the relative effect of
an airplane at a given weight on a pavement structure for a specified subgrade strength
PCN is a number that identifies the load carrying capacity of pavement for unrestricted
operations The ACN PCN system is structured so that a pavement with a particular
PCN value can support without weight restrictions an airplane that has an ACN value
equal to or less than the pavement PCN value
The FAA computer program COMFAA was used to estimate the ACN values for
the aircraft types that are expected to operate in the project site area However the
official ACN values provided by the airplane manufacturers should be used for
analyses The following table summarizes the calculated ACN values

As shown on the table the PCN values for the existing TL G TL L and
TL G L IIT500 pavements were lower than the ACN values for the design aircraft
Therefore the forecasted aircraft mix cannot be operated on these pavement areas
without strengthening improvement In accordance with the ACN PCN evaluation the
pavements without restrictions is B717 which has an ACN value of 32 and the TL L
pavement can only be used by commuter planes
Since the design aircraft for the pavement is B777 300ER the new AC pavement
or the existing pavement with AC overlay would have a PCN of at least 71 after the
rehabilitation construction Therefore the improved pavements would be capable to
support the B747 and A380 aircrafts which have ACN of 69 and 62 respectively

SECTION 11
APPROACH AND METHODOLOGY
Analyses
The FAA computer program FAARFIELD Airport Pavement Design V1 302
was used to perform pavement design analysis using the FAA elastic layered design
approach The FAARFIELD pavement design program automatically assigned modulus
P 209 materials and these values by default cannot be changed The results of the
FAARFIELD analysis are presented on Plates F 1 through F 6 in Appendix F
Analyses
For checking and comparison purposes an alternative AC overlay design using
the mechanistic elastic layer analysis approach was conducted to develop the
AC overlay thickness by using the computer program NLAYER The mechanistic
method has been in use since the late 1960 s and much experience has been gained in
its use for analysis and design of heavy duty pavements The procedures expand the
single base subgrade layer into separate layers closely resembling the actual
subsurface conditions Using the deflection values under the normalized 40 kip load
the NLAYER program was used to back calculate the moduli of AC base material and
subgrade soils and the results are presented in Appendix G on Plates G 1 and G 2
and on Plates G 7 and G 8 for TL G and IIT apron respectively
The primary AC pavement failure mode is fatigue cracking which is related to
strain at the bottom of the asphalt concrete layer Using the back calculated material
moduli and the design aircraft wheel load the NLAYER program was then used to
estimate the resulting principal strain from different AC overlay thicknesses The
relationship between strain E1 and allowable repetitions N for airfield pavement is
log N 20 log E1 for psi
Once the allowable repetitions were calculated the design life can be determined
by dividing the allowable repetitions by the annual departures of the design aircraft The
SECTION 11 APPROACH AND METHODOLOGY
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
Page 24
Hawaii California
results of the NLAYER analyses for AC overlay on existing AC pavement are presented
END OF APPROACH AND METHODOLOGY

As shown on the table the use of B777 aircraft as full replacement of the A350
aircraft would result in a slightly thicker pavement structural section For the pavement
evaluation and analyses discussed below the A350 aircrafts were completely replaced
by the for a slightly conservative pavement design
L
In accordance with FAA Advisory Circular the minimum width of a
taxilane should be 75 feet and the minimum separation centerline distance between
two adjacent parallel taxilanes should be at least 245 feet for airplane design Group V
The current separation between Taxilanes G and L is less than the 245 foot
requirement Therefore the new centerline of Taxilane L would need to be relocated to
at least the existing taxilane shoulder area Even for the interim design Taxilane L
should be reconstructed
For a 20 year design life the recommended new pavement structural section is
crushed aggregate base P 209 This pavement design was based on a subgrade
CBR of about 15 equivalent to a subgrade modulus of 22 500 psi It is our opinion that
SECTION 12 PAVEMENT DESIGN RECOMMENDATIONS
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
Page 26
Hawaii California
the coralline silty sand material encountered at the site should meet this criterion if the
material is compacted to a relative compaction of at least 95 percent based on ASTM D
1557 The FAARFIELD analysis results are presented on Plate F 3
G
If the centerline of Taxilane G could remain approximately at its present
alignment then an AC overlay construction would be feasible for interim design before
the final taxilane straightening construction The FAARFIELD analysis results on
Plate F 4 indicated an AC overlay thickness of 9 3 inches and the NLAYER results on
Plates G 3 through G 6 showed 12 inches of AC overlay for a 20 year design life We
recommend a minimum AC overlay thickness of 9 5 inches for a 20 year design life
Depending on the construction schedule and project budget the Airport may
consider reconstructing TL G with new pavement using the recommended new
pavement sections presented above for TL L To avoid extending the construction into
Taxiway A the pavement in the intersection area would need to be reconstructed In
addition in the conformed area to the existing IIT apron pavement new pavement
construction will likely be required in order to meet grades
Prior to the AC overlay construction the existing pavement surface should be
cleared of all loose materials and debris Linear pavement cracks of ¼ inch or greater
in width should be prepared for filling by using high pressure air or water to remove
vegetation and foreign material The cleaned cracks should then be filled with a
commercially available sand asphalt emulsion material The existing asphalt concrete
pavement in the badly alligator cracked and distorted rutted areas should be identified
before construction The full thickness of these failed pavement areas should be
completely removed at least 12 inches laterally beyond the failure zone by grinding or
sawcutting and excavating The exposed subgrade should then be proof rolled If soft
subgrade is identified the yielding subgrade area should be excavated for an additional
depth of 6 inches and replaced with new asphalt concrete along with the remainder of
the overexcavated pavement area to the top of the existing pavement grades
deep patching
SECTION 12 PAVEMENT DESIGN RECOMMENDATIONS
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
Page 27
Hawaii California
We understand that reflective cracking from the existing pavement is a concern
In general reflective cracking is more evident in HMA AC overlays on PCC
pavements The general rule is that development of reflective cracking would be
delayed by one year per inch of AC overlay thickness Therefore for the TL G
pavement the reflective cracking could be masked by about 9 to 10 years Fractured
slab technology such as crack break and seat and rubblization has been used as an
alternative to total reconstruction However it may not be necessary because reflective
cracking from AC pavement is not as big of an issue as from jointed PCC pavement If
the subgrade is in good condition rubblization would reduce the modulus of the
supporting layer from old AC to aggregate base and thereby increase the required AC
overlay thickness If the subgrade is in questionable condition the existing pavement
would need to be removed so that the subgrade can be evaluated The advantage of
in place rubblization would be diminished Therefore it is our opinion that rubblization
is not a cost effective alternative for the TL G rehabilitation
In the 500 foot long area north of the mauka end of TL G L see Plate 2 we
recommend pavement reconstruction or AC overlay The recommendations presented
in Subsection 12 3 above for TL G should be followed
Unlike TL G and TL L that could be shut down one at a time for the proposed
construction the TL G L IIT500 pavement is located in the bottleneck area of Gates 49
through 51 of the Interisland Terminal and the new Mauka Concourse Construction
staging would be a major challenge Only one new boring was drilled in this area We
recommend conducting additional field exploration and in situ testing in this area for the
final design to further explore the pavement and subsurface conditions in order to
fine tune the pavement design
The NLAYER analysis results on Plate G 9 indicated that the existing AC taxilane
and apron area directly in front of the Interisland Terminal should have a remaining life
of about 6 years based on the aircraft traffic schedule for Year 2018 Since the aircraft
SECTION 12 PAVEMENT DESIGN RECOMMENDATIONS
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
Page 28
Hawaii California
traffic would be less between now and 2018 the remaining pavement life should be at
least 10 years Depending on the actual aircraft traffic and travel pattern the existing
IIT AC pavement could have a remaining life of more than 10 years Therefore
improvement of the existing TL G L IIT apron pavement is not necessary at this time
Pavement analyses were performed using FAARFIELD and the results
as shown on Plate F 5 indicated an AC overlay thickness of about 3 inches for a
20 year design life The NLAYER results Plates G 10 and G 11 also showed an
overlay thickness of 3 inches Therefore if the Airport would like to bring the design life
of the pavement back to 20 years we recommend a minimum AC overlay thickness of
3 inches Please note that if the pavement overlay rehabilitation work will be done in
the future the required AC overlay thickness would be thicker If the pavement is
allowed to completely deteriorate overlay construction may not be feasible and the only
option would be replacement and re construction
Terminal Apron
Our field exploration conducted in the existing IIT apron area encountered about
14 5 inch PCC and 4 inch ACSB over granular subgrade material This existing PCC
pavement was judged to be in good condition with a PCI of about 97 in 2005
APTI 2007 The pavement was constructed in 1995 Therefore the existing PCC
pavement has been in service for about 15 years We assume that the average aircraft
traffic for the past 15 years was about half of the estimated Year 2018 traffic
Using the back calculated modulus of for the PCC the flexural
strength was calculated to be about 610 psi With these parameters FAARFIELD
estimated a cumulative damage factor used CDFU of about 35 percent in the past
15 years With the forecasted Year 2018 aircraft traffic FAARFIELD estimated a
pavement life of 14 years for a CDFU of about 65 percent Therefore the remaining life
of the existing PCC pavement would be about 14 years when the percent CDFU is
equal to 100 The FAARFIELD analysis results are presented on Plate F 6
SECTION 12 PAVEMENT DESIGN RECOMMENDATIONS
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
Page 29
Hawaii California
12 7 Pavement Materials
Bituminous materials quality requirements and construction procedures should
conform to the FAA specification Item P 401 Plant Mix Bituminous Pavements The
aggregate should meet the gradation requirements for one inch maximum and the
asphalt binder should be It is recommended that the coarse and fine
aggregate should be 100 percent crushed with a minimum sand equivalent of 50
Reclaimed asphalt pavement RAP should not be allowed
Portland cement concrete PCC should meet the requirements of P 501
Portland Cement Concrete Pavement and should have a minimum flexural strength of
650 psi at 28 day
Asphalt concrete stabilized base ACSB should meet the requirements of
P 401 P 403 except the aggregate gradation should meet the requirements on Table
of the Hawaii DOT specifications which is similar to the gradation of the FAA
1½ inch Max This modification is suggested because local contractors can more
readily produce the HDOT gradation due to the large volumes of State highway
construction All other aggregate quality requirements on P 401 P 403 would apply It
is recommended that asphalt binder be and no more than 15 percent RAP be
used in the ACSB mix
Crushed aggregate base CAB if used should meet the requirements of P 209
Crushed Aggregate Base Course The base materials should be compacted in
accordance with the requirements of P 209 based on test method ASTM D1557
12 8 Quality Control
Quality control on Hot Mix Asphalt HMA AC construction is critical to the
long term performance of the asphalt pavements regardless of overlay construction or
new AC pavement construction Asphalt concrete pavement should be constructed in
strict accordance with FAA P 401 especially under Section related to mat
density and joint density
SECTION 12 PAVEMENT DESIGN RECOMMENDATIONS
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
Page 30
Hawaii California
The paving contractor should submit a job mix formula JMF prepared by an
approved independent material testing laboratory having at least 10 years of experience
in designing asphalt concrete mix The JMF should not be more than 90 days old at the
time of the submittal and should have been produced with samples of materials
submitted by the contractor to be used on the project not from stock materials the
laboratory has on hand
Asphalt concrete should be placed in lifts of no more than 3 to 4 inches thick
each but should be no thinner than three times the nominal maximum size aggregate
Thicker asphalt concrete layers should be placed in two or more lifts of approximately
equal thickness In addition asphalt concrete should be compacted to meet the
requirements of Section

and should not be used at this time for final design The grading plans of the project

the final site grading plan the project layout and new taxilane alignment are available
so that the pavement design recommendations can be finalized

APPENDIX A
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
MAY 2010 Page A 1
Hawaii California
A P P E N D I X A
Field Exploration
We explored the subsurface conditions at the project site by drilling and sampling
twenty two borings corings designated as Boring Nos 1 through 22 The borings
extended to depths of about 4 to 5 feet below the existing pavement surface at selected
locations The approximate boring locations are shown on the Overall Boring Location
Plan Plate 3 and on the Boring Location Plans Plates 4 1 and 4 2 The borings were
drilled using a truck mounted drill rig equipped with continuous flight augers and coring
tools
Our geologist classified the materials encountered in the borings by visual and
textural examination in the field in general accordance with ASTM D 2488 Standard
Practice for Description and Identification of Soils and monitored the drilling operations on
a near continuous full time basis These classifications were further reviewed visually
and by testing in the laboratory Soils were classified in general accordance with
ASTM D 2487 Standard Practice for Classification of Soils for Engineering Purposes
Unified Soil Classification System as shown on the Soil Log Legend Plate A Graphic
representations of the materials encountered are presented on the Logs of Borings
Plates A 1 through A 22
Relatively undisturbed soil samples were obtained in general accordance with
ASTM D 3550 Ring Lined Barrel Sampling of Soils by driving a 3 inch OD Modified
California sampler with a 140 pound hammer falling 30 inches In addition some samples
were obtained from the drilled borings in general accordance with ASTM D 1586
Penetration Test and Split Barrel Sampling of Soils by driving a 2 inch OD standard
penetration sampler using the same hammer and drop The blow counts needed to drive
the sampler the second and third 6 inches of an 18 inch drive are shown as the
Penetration Resistance on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths The
penetration resistance shown on the Logs of Borings indicates the number of blows
required for the specific sampler type used The blow counts may need to be factored to
obtain the Standard Penetration Test SPT blow counts
Pocket penetrometer tests were performed on selected cohesive soil samples in
the field The pocket penetrometer test provides an indication of the unconfined
compressive strength of the sample Results of the pocket penetrometer tests are
summarized on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths

Plate A 24
APPENDIX B
W O 6213 00
GEOLABS INC
MAY 2010 Page B 1
Hawaii California
A P P E N D I X B
Laboratory Tests
Moisture Content ASTM D 2216 and Unit Weight ASTM D 2937 determinations
were performed on selected samples as an aid in the classification and evaluation of soil
properties The test results are presented on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate
sample depths
Four Atterberg Limits tests ASTM D 4318 were performed on selected soil
samples to evaluate the liquid and plastic limits The test results are summarized on the
Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths Graphic presentations of the test results
are provided on Plate B 1
Twelve Sieve Analysis tests were performed on selected
soil samples to evaluate the gradation characteristics of the soils and to aid in soil
classification Graphic presentations of the test results are provided on Plates B 2 through
B 4
Four laboratory California Bearing Ratio tests ASTM D 1883 were performed on
bulk samples of the near surface soils to evaluate the pavement support characteristics of
the soils The test results are presented on Plates B 5 through B 8

Task 3 NDT Data Analysis
In Task 3 the FWD data was analyzed The overall purpose of the data analysis
is to assess the strength characteristics of the pavement layers and subgrade
identify the remaining structural life years and identify structural improvements
for structurally deficient pavement sections The results were analyzed using the
results from the records review and additional geotechnical data that is collected
as part of the study by Geo Labs The following parameters were analyzed
Normalized Maximum FWD Deflections
API has analyzed the maximum FWD deflections deflection occurring directly
under the FWD load plate to identify pavement strength and assess the overall
support conditions of the in place pavements
Pavement deflection profile plots are helpful in characterizing the response of
each pavement cross section for the entire length of each facility tested The
maximum FWD deflections normalized to a standard load have been plotted
against the stationing of each pavement section Changes in a pavement s
structural capacity or current condition are easily identified by abrupt changes in
the deflection profile Profile plots of maximum deflections can be found in
Attachment 2 Impulse Stiffness Modulus Plots These plots also assist in the
selection of isolated pavement sections that are structurally weaker than
surrounding pavement This information was used by API to assist Geo Labs in
identifying locally weak areas where additional cores should be retrieved to assist
in the structural evaluation
Back calculation of Pavement Layer Strengths
A pavement layer strength analysis has been performed to identify the stiffness
strength of AC and PCC layers the base courses beneath these surface layers
and the underlying subgrade support The pavement layer strength analysis was
performed using a technique called backcalculation The backcalculated
modulus values may be examined to draw some conclusions about the degree of
structural deterioration in the pavement layers and the expected remaining life of
the pavement
The general principle of backcalculation is that the deflections measured by the
FWD are dependent on the following parameters
W O 6213 00
PLATE D 5
6
Hawaii Office 94 1221 Ka Uka Blvd Unit 108 Waipahu HI 96797
Thickness and stiffness of the pavement layers
Stiffness of the foundation
Degree of friction between the pavement layers
Magnitude of applied load
Radius of applied load
Rate of loading
Position of the deflection sensors
If the loading deflection and layer thickness parameters are known and the
interface friction condition is assumed the measured pavement deflections may
be used to solve for the stiffness of the pavement layers and foundation
The results of the backcalculation can be found in Attachment 3 2009 Pavement
Evaluation Summary Backcalculation Results
An overview of the results can be seen as follows

PCC Joint Load Transfer Efficiency
The analysis of PCC joints or cracks is very important because the amount of
load that is transferred from one PCC slab to the adjacent slab can significantly
impact the structural capacity of the pavement Figure 4 illustrates the concept of
joint or crack load transfer
Figure 4 Illustration of load transfer across a PCC joint or crack
Aircraft Wheel
Loaded PCC Slab
Unloaded PCC Slab
W O 6213 00
PLATE D 8
9
Hawaii Office 94 1221 Ka Uka Blvd Unit 108 Waipahu HI 96797
API used the NDT joint deflection data to determine what percentage of the total
main gear weight of an aircraft is transferred from the loaded slab to the
unloaded slab As the amount of load that is transferred to the unloaded slab
increases the flexural stress in the loaded slab decreases and the pavement life
is extended The amount of load transfer depends on many factors including
gear configuration tire contact area pavement temperature use of dowel bars
and use of a stabilized base beneath the PCC surface layer
In general the joint load transfer efficiency of the PCC joints of the sections
acceptable values There was more variation of the joint load transfer efficiency
of the PCC joints of the section 84 The joint load
transfer efficiency is shown graphically in Attachment 5 PCC Joint Load Transfer
Efficiency Plots According to the FAA Advisory Circular load transfer is
considered to be good when it is greater 70 Most of the sections on the
Terminal Ramp have good load transfer although the PCC GA ramp average
load transfer is approaching 50 percent When the LTE falls below 50 percent
only a small percentage of the main gear wheel load is transferred to the
adjacent slab as the wheel approaches the common joint between the loaded
and unloaded slabs
The primary objective of a network level structural analysis is to determine the load
carrying capacity of each pavement section If a pavement section is structurally
deficient it will not be able to carry the projected aircraft traffic operations for the
intended remaining life of the section For structurally deficient sections a rehabilitation
alternative must be designed that will provide the intended service life The preferred
structural repairs are one of many inputs that are considered in the development of a
multi year CIP
There are several approaches that can be used to determine if a pavement section is
structurally deficient Questions that can be asked might include
Which aircraft causes the most structural damage i e Critical Aircraft
If a pavement section will not support aircraft operations for at least the next
20 years how many aircraft departures can it support i e Allowable Annual
Passes
Alternatively if a pavement section will not support expected aircraft
operations for at least the next 20 years what reduction in traffic volume or
W O 6213 00
PLATE D 9
10
Hawaii Office 94 1221 Ka Uka Blvd Unit 108 Waipahu HI 96797
load is necessary to support traffic for 20 years of operations i e Allowable
Aircraft Weight
What is the relative load carrying capacity of the pavement sections runways
taxiways with respect to the actual loads that are applied i e ACN PCN as
defined within this section
API used PCASE to answer these structural questions PCASE has been integrated
into the MicroPAVER software that was developed by the U S Army Corps of
Engineers and allows for simple data transfer between the two software tools to perform
this analysis
It is important to note that a network level structural analysis is performed based on 100
percent of the maximum gross take off weight MGTOW
Since this a Project level design in accordance with 5320 6E and its
associated design software API used 100 percent of MGTOW for
rehabilitation designs When the detailed project level analysis work was conducted for
each pavement section the traffic data assigned to the pavement section was reviewed
and structural design alternatives were determined using PCASE and FAARFIELD
Structural inputs that are required in the analysis are as follows
Pavement layer thicknesses and material types
Amount of load related PCI distresses
Current and projected fleet mix operations
Pavement layer strengths
Both PCASE and FAARFIELD use mechanistic analysis procedures in pavement
evaluation and design Each program uses an elastic layer analysis to determine the
structural capacity of each pavement the expected remaining life in years and the
improvements that are required for structurally deficient pavements The elastic layer
analysis computes the response of the pavement in terms of stresses strains and
deflections that occur from aircraft traffic loads For this evaluation horizontal tensile
strains at the bottom of the AC layer and flexural stress at the bottom of the PCC layer
were used to compute the allowable number of aircraft departures for each pavement
W O 6213 00
PLATE D 10
11
Hawaii Office 94 1221 Ka Uka Blvd Unit 108 Waipahu HI 96797
section In addition the vertical compressive strain on the subgrade was checked to be
sure that the fine grained subgrade would not rut under projected traffic
CRITICAL AIRCRAFT
The design aircraft or critical aircraft is determined in accordance with FAA AC
The design aircraft is the aircraft that requires the greatest pavement
thickness when designing for the forecast number of annual departures It is not
necessarily the heaviest aircraft In the case of the Inter Island Terminal pavements
projected traffic forecast provided by GeoLabs the design aircraft is generally the
ANNUAL ALLOWABLE PASSES AND ALLOWABLE AIRCRAFT WEIGHT
For PCC pavements structural failure is typically defined as that point in time when 50
percent of the PCC slabs on average have at least one fatigue related crack The
allowable number of aircraft coverages for a PCC pavement was determined by PCASE
and FAARFIELD when the projected aircraft fleet mix is operating at 100 percent of the
MGTOW The allowable number of coverages is converted to the number of allowable
passes using Equation 2
PASS COV P C
eq 1
where
PASS
Number of allowable passes departures to failure
P C
Aircraft pass to coverage ratio for its main gear
COV
Number of coverages to failure
The P C ratios are automatically calculated in FAARFIELD and PCASE for channelized
e g taxiways and unchannelized e g aprons and runway interior aircraft traffic
Once the allowable number of coverages and P C ratios are determined the product of
these two results provides the allowable number of departures passes for a pavement
section
Since there are several heavy aircraft in a fleet mix the cumulative effect of departures
of several aircraft models is determined through Miner s fatigue consumption damage
model which results in the predicted number of future allowable passes that can occur
before pavement failure The Miner s model assumes that structural damage is
W O 6213 00
PLATE D 11
12
Hawaii Office 94 1221 Ka Uka Blvd Unit 108 Waipahu HI 96797
cumulative and that a pavement has a finite life defined by the allowable number of load
applications prior to failure Each load application consumes a small percentage of the
pavement life When load applications equal the number allowable Miner s damage is
equal to 1 0 and significant structural deterioration has occurred Past damage values
less than 1 0 are expected for pavements that exhibit lower amounts of fatigue cracking
e g load related PCI distresses Equation 2 illustrates Miner s theory
Where
ni Number of past aircraft passes i
Ni Number of past allowable aircraft passes i
based on existing cross section
nj Number of future aircraft passes j
Nj Number of future allowable aircraft passes j
based on structural improvements
The amount of fatigue damage depends on the amount of past and future traffic
Reliable past aircraft traffic data is seldom available for pavement structural evaluations
so in order to account for past fatigue damage PCASE reduces the load carrying
capacity of pavements by 25 percent when the PCI value is below 40
The process used for AC pavements is very similar to the process that is used for PCC
sections However rather than using a model that predicts the number of allowable
coverages for the PCC layer based on the Modulus of Rupture the allowable number
of coverages is based on fatigue models for the subgrade and the AC surface layer
Miner s model is also used for the AC pavement analysis
ACN PCN System
The ACN PCN is a system for reporting the airfield pavement bearing strength For this
system the analysis is conducted according to the International Civil Aviation
Organization ICAO as outlined in the Doc 9157 AN 901 Part 3 Aerodrome Design
Manual and the FAA Advisory Circular 5A titled Standardized Method of
Reporting Airport Pavement Strength PCN The definitions of aircraft classification
number ACN and pavement classification number PCN as presented in AC
are as follows
Miner s Damage
n
N
n
N

eq 2
W O 6213 00
PLATE D 12
13
Hawaii Office 94 1221 Ka Uka Blvd Unit 108 Waipahu HI 96797
ACN
A number that expresses the relative structural effect of an aircraft on different
pavement types for specified standard subgrade strengths in terms of a
standard single wheel load The ACN number is calculated using aircrafts
characteristics
PCN
A number that expresses the relative load carrying capacity of a pavement in
terms of a standard single wheel load
The ACN PCN system is structured so a pavement with a particular PCN value can
support without weight restrictions an airplane that has an ACN value equal to or less
than the pavement s PCN value This is possible because ACN and PCN values are
computed using the same theory
Aircraft Classification Number ACN
The ACN represents the relative structural impact of a particular aircraft on different
pavement types for specified standard subgrade strengths This is accomplished by
expressing the required pavement thickness for an individual aircraft in terms of the
equivalent single wheel load that would be needed to produce that same pavement
thickness see Figure 5 The magnitude of the equivalent single wheel load expressed
in Mg multiplied by two is the ACN Higher ACN values indicate the aircraft would have
a more severe effect on a pavement whereas lower values indicate a less severe
effect However granular subgrade soils with higher CBR s and thus stronger support
conditions will produce a lower ACN than those with weaker support conditions ACN s
have a minimum value of 0 with no upper limit

AC pavements and shows the ACN values that will occur for the four subgrade strength
categories The weakest subgrade category is D where the CBR value is less than
four and strongest subgrade category is A where the CBR value is greater than 13

Pavement Classification Number PCN
The PCN is established by a structural assessment of the pavement and indicates a
pavement s ability to support without weight restrictions an aircraft that has an ACN
value equal to or less than the PCN value This number is applicable to both flexible and
rigid pavements The PCN value also includes codes following the numerical value that
present additional information from the following categories
Pavement Type
R Rigid
F Flexible
Subgrade Strength Category
A High k 440 psi in or CBR 13
B Medium k 220 to 440 psi in or CBR 8 to 13

Pavement Evaluation Method
T Technical Evaluation
N Non technical Evaluation
For the analysis work on the HNL Inter Island Terminal pavements pavement type is
AC for AC and AC overlaid AC pavements and PCC for PCC pavements the PCN
values are reported for the subgrade strength category maximum allowable tire
pressure is W unlimited and the pavement evaluation is T because a technical
evaluation was conducted using for taxiway and apron pavement sections
Evaluation of ACN PCN Results
The ACN PCN system is structured such that a pavement with a given PCN value can
support an aircraft that has an ACN value equal to or less than the PCN Likewise the
pavement cannot accept an aircraft with an ACN value exceeding the PCN However
the Aerodrome Design Manual does allow for some overloads according to the following
criteria ICAO
For flexible pavements occasional movements by aircraft with an ACN not
exceeding 10 percent of the reported PCN should not adversely affect the
pavement
For rigid or composite pavements occasional movements by aircraft with an
ACN not exceeding 5 percent of the reported ACN should not adversely affect
the pavement
The annual number of overload movements should not exceed approximately
5 percent of the total annual aircraft movements
To evaluate the load carrying capability of the airport pavement sections using the ACN
PCN procedure the section PCN values are compared with the ACN of the aircraft
using that section The most typical use of the ACN PCN procedure is to determine
W O 6213 00
PLATE D 16
17
Hawaii Office 94 1221 Ka Uka Blvd Unit 108 Waipahu HI 96797
whether a particular aircraft type not currently using the Inter Island Terminal
pavements at HNL can or should be allowed to use one of the facilities
It should be noted that another means of structural evaluation according to the
ACN PCN method i e the non technical method only investigates the ACN of the
aircraft currently using an airfield If the aircraft has not caused damage to the airfield
pavement then the PCN of that facility is assumed to be at least as great as the aircraft
using the facility This method is not recommended because a comprehensive structural
evaluation was conducted at HNL
Limitations of the ACN PCN Procedure
Regardless of whether a technical or non technical evaluation method has been used to
assign PCN values to a pavement section it is important to understand the limitation of
the ACN PCN system As stated in the FAA Advisory Circular The PCN
value is for reporting relative pavement strength only and should not be used for
pavement design or as a substitute for evaluation Pavement design and evaluation are
complex engineering problems that require detailed analyses They cannot be reduced
to a single number The PCN rating system uses a continuous scale to compare
pavement strength where higher values represent pavements with larger load capacity
The pavement sections were analyzed to determine their load carrying capacity as
described within this letter report The results are presented in Attachment 6 2009
Pavement Evaluation Summary Remaining Structural Life along with the PCN
results In addition the ACN to PCN ratios were computed using PCASE When the
ACN PCN ratio is below 1 1 no pavement sections are being overloaded
It should be noted that it is believed that the rutting and surface deformation observed
on Taxiways L and G are being caused by a mix design and stability deficiencies in
the AC surface It is also believed that a Hawaii State Mix is being used instead of the
FAA P 401 airfield mix It was observed that the majority of the current traffic is from
Hawaiian B 737 Inter Island flights This type of aircraft would not typically produce the
rutting and surface deformations that were observed for the existing pavement cross
section if a higher quality AC mix had been used during construction Therefore it is
recommended that additional laboratory testing and a volumetric analysis be performed
in order to determine the exact reasons for the instability in the AC surface mix on these
two taxiways
